Human Perfection: Theoretical and Practical Powers
Razi defines human perfection as two powers: theoretical and practical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's definition of human perfection as the integration of both theoretical and practical powers, and why both are necessary for true happiness.

Core Idea
Human perfection requires both perfect knowledge and righteous action.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi lays out the foundational principle that human perfection is not merely theoretical but also practical. He defines two distinct powers that must be fully developed: the theoretical power to perceive realities without error, and the practical power to perform righteous deeds that turn the soul toward the afterlife. This preliminary is presented as universally accepted by prophets and philosophers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage in Arabic and English
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation to get a clear sense of Razi's definition.
Notice the strong claim of consensus at the end.
2
Identify the two powers
The passage presents two components of perfection: theoretical power (قوة نظرية) and practical power (قوة عملية). Find how each is described.
3
Understand righteous deeds
Razi defines righteous deeds as those that cause aversion to bodily pleasures and desire for the afterlife. This sets the direction for human perfection.
This definition is key to understanding the practical power.
4
Reflect on the consensus claim
Razi states that prophets and divine philosophers agree on this preliminary. Ask yourself: why is this point considered universally accepted?
This reinforces the importance of the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأجل العمل به، والمراد منه : أن كمال حاله محصور في أمرين : أحدهما : أن تصير قوته النظرية كاملة بحيث تتجلى فيها صور الأشياء وحقائقها ، تجلياً كاملاً تاماً ، مبرأ عن الخطأ والزلل . والثاني : أن تصير قوته العملية كاملة بحيث يحصل لصاحبها ملكة ، يقدر بها على الإتيان بالأعمال الصالحة . والمراد من الأعمال الصالحة : الأحوال التي توجب النفرة عن السعادات البدنية ، وتوجب الرغبة في عالم الآخرة ، وفي الروحانيات . فقد ظهر بهذا : أن لا سعادة للإنسان إلا بالوصول إلى هاتين الحالتين. وهذه المقدمة مقدمة أطبق الأنبياء على صحتها ، واتفق الحكماء الإلهيون على صحتها أيضاً، ولا يرى في الدنيا عاقل كامل ، إلا ويساعد عليها .
For the sake of acting by it, and what is intended from it: that his perfect state is restricted to two matters: one of them: that his theoretical power becomes complete such that images and realities of things are manifested in it, a complete, perfect manifestation, free from error and slip. And the second: that his practical power becomes complete such that its possessor acquires a faculty by which he is able to perform righteous deeds. And what is meant by righteous deeds: the states that cause aversion to bodily pleasures and cause desire for the world of the hereafter and for spiritualities. So it has become clear by this: that there is no happiness for man except by reaching these two states. And this preliminary is a preliminary upon which the prophets unanimously agreed on its correctness, and the divine philosophers also agreed on its correctness, and there is no complete rational person in the world except he supports it.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage defines the first preliminary (muqaddamah) about human perfection. It states that perfect human state is confined to two things: the theoretical power becoming perfect so that the forms and realities of things are manifested in it completely, free from error; and the practical power becoming perfect through a faculty enabling righteous deeds, which are defined as those that turn away from bodily pleasures and increase desire for the afterlife and spiritual matters. From these two, the author concludes that human happiness (sa'adah) is only attained by achieving both states. He then emphasizes that this preliminary is unanimously accepted by prophets and divine philosophers, and every complete rational person supports it.

Key Terms
قوة نظرية
قوة عملية
كمال
سعادة
أعمال صالحة
الحكماء الإلهيون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think human perfection is only about theoretical knowledge or intellectual achievement.
Correction: Razi clearly states that perfection requires both theoretical and practical powers. Righteous deeds that turn away from bodily pleasures are equally essential.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the meaning of 'righteous deeds' (al-a'mal al-salihah)?
A) Deeds that are performed publicly
B) Deeds that cause aversion to bodily pleasures and desire for the afterlife
C) Deeds that increase wealth and status
D) Deeds that are approved by the majority

Key Takeaway
True human perfection is achieved only when both the theoretical power (knowledge of realities) and practical power (righteous action) are fully developed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of the two powers—theoretical or practical—do you find yourself neglecting most often? What is one concrete action you can take today to strengthen the weaker one?
?
Think of your daily routine: does it increase your craving for bodily pleasures or your desire for the hereafter? If the former, what righteous deed (as Razi defines it) can you perform tomorrow to shift your heart toward the spiritual?
?
The prophets and all divine philosophers agreed that happiness depends on both knowledge and action. Are you living as if you believe this? If not, what is the first change you will make starting now?

The Infinitude of Degrees of Perfection and Deficiency
Razi's third preliminary on the infinitude of degrees.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the claim that the degrees of perfection and deficiency in both theoretical and practical powers are virtually infinite and known by necessity.

Core Idea
Perfection and deficiency have infinite degrees, and this is self-evident.

Before Razi can argue for a single highest degree of perfection, he needs to establish that degrees themselves are unlimited. This short but crucial passage asserts that perfections and deficiencies in both intellect and action have no finite bound — a fact he says is known by intuition. Let's read it carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the phrasing 'as if infinite' — it signals a conceptual rather than literal claim.
The phrase 'as if' softens the claim; it means 'effectively infinite' or 'beyond finite enumeration.'
2
Identify the two domains
The passage mentions two powers: theoretical (intellectual) and practical (moral/action). Degrees of perfection and deficiency apply to both.
3
Understand 'known by necessity'
Razi says this is 'ma‘lum bi al-ḍarūrah' — known by necessary, intuitive knowledge. Ask yourself: Is it self-evident that there can always be a greater or lesser degree of something?
This is a foundational premise; it doesn't require proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثالثة : إن درجات النقصان والكمال في القوة النظرية ، وفي القوة العملية كأنها غير متناهية بحسب الشدة والضعف والقلة والكثرة . وذلك أيضاً معلوم بالضرورة .
The third preliminary: Verily the degrees of deficiency and perfection in the theoretical power and in the practical power are as if infinite in terms of intensity and weakness and fewness and multitude. And that too is known by necessity.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This very short passage states the third preliminary: the degrees of deficiency and perfection in both theoretical and practical powers are virtually infinite ('as if infinite') in intensity, weakness, fewness, and multitude. The author asserts that this is known by necessity (intuitively self-evident). This prepares for the argument that a single perfect person must exist at the highest degree.

Key Terms
درجات
النقصان
الكمال
غير متناهية
بالضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that degrees of perfection and deficiency are finite or fixed, like a limited number of ranks.
Correction: Razi asserts that these degrees are 'as if infinite' — not literally infinite but effectively unlimited; one can always conceive of a greater or lesser degree. This is known intuitively.

Checkpoint
According to the third preliminary, how are the degrees of perfection and deficiency described?
A) They are fixed and limited
B) They are as if infinite in intensity and quantity
C) They are known only through scripture
D) They are exactly the same for all people

Key Takeaway
The third preliminary establishes that degrees of perfection and deficiency are virtually infinite, both in intensity and quantity, and this is intuitively known.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that there are infinite degrees of deficiency you could sink to and infinite degrees of perfection you could strive for, how would that change your daily efforts? Are you content with your current degree, or do you see the infinite ladder before you? What specific step will you take today to ascend one degree?
?
Razi says this is self-evident. But do you live as if perfection is infinite, or do you settle for mediocrity? What is one area of your life where you have stopped striving because you thought you had reached enough?
?
The passage mentions both theoretical and practical powers. Which of the two is more lacking in your own life right now? What is one concrete action you can take this week to increase your perfection in that domain, knowing that any increase is just one step on an infinite path?

How Dreams Arise: The Soul's Connection and the Imaginative Faculty
Razi describes the mechanism of dream formation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's explanation of how dreams are produced through the soul's connection to higher principles and the subsequent activity of the imaginative faculty.

Core Idea
Dreams are the soul's natural reception of forms, translated into images by the imaginative faculty.

In this passage, Razi begins by describing the psychological process that underlies dreaming. He explains how the soul, when temporarily freed from bodily management, connects to higher principles and receives abstract forms relevant to its own concerns. These forms are then translated into sensory images by the imaginative faculty, which appear in the common sense as dreams. This lesson focuses on that foundational process.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the sequence: soul respite → connection to principles → forms impression → imaginative faculty depiction → common sense.
Pay attention to the terms: النفس الناطقة, القوة المتخيلة, الحس المشترك.
2
Identify the key faculties
The passage mentions three key faculties: the rational soul, the imaginative faculty, and the common sense. Understand their roles in the dream process.
The imaginative faculty is described as 'imitating' the abstract forms.
3
Trace the causal chain
Write down the step-by-step process from the soul's detachment to the observable dream. Ensure you see why each step is necessary.
The soul naturally connects to principles when free from bodily tasks.
4
Reflect on the suitability of forms
Razi says the forms most suitable to the soul relate to the person's own affairs or interests. Consider why this makes sense for dreams.
This explains why dreams often reflect our personal concerns.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : النفس إذا حصل لها أدنى فراغ من تدبير البدن ، اتصلت بطباعها بتلك المبادىء ، فتنطبع فيها بعض تلك الصور الحاضرة عند تلك المبادىء ، وهي الصورة التي هي أليق بتلك النفس . ومعلوم أن أليق الأحوال بها ، ما يتعلق بأحوال ذلك الإنسان وبأصحابه وأهل بلده وإقليمه . وأما إن كان ذلك الإنسان منجذب الهمة إلى تحصيل علوم المعقولات ، لاحت له منها أشياء . ومن كانت همته مصالح الناس رآها ، ثم إذا انطبعت تلك الصور في جوهر النفس الناطقة أخذت المتخيلة التي من طباعها محاكاة الأمور ، في حكاية تلك الصور المنطبعة في النفس ، بصور جزئية تناسبها ، ثم إن تلك الصور تنطبع في الحس المشترك فتصير مشاهدة. فهذا هو سبب الرؤيا في المنام . ثم إن تلك الصور التي ركبتها القوة المتخيلة ، لأجل تلك المعاني قد تكون شديدة المناسبة لتلك المعاني ، فتكون هذه الرؤيا غنية عن التعبير وقد لا تكون كذلك ، إلا أنها أيضاً مناسبة لتلك المعاني من بعض الوجوه ، وههنا تحتاج هذه المنامات إلى التعبير . وفائدة التعبير : التحليل بالعكس ، يعني : أن المعبر عن هذه الصور الحاضرة في الخيال إلى تلك المعاني وأما القسم الثالث أن لا تكون هذه الصور مناسبة لتلك المعاني البتة . وذلك يكون لأحد وجهين : أحدهما : أن يكون حدوث هذا الخيال الغريب ، إنما كان لوجه من الوجوه الثلاثة المذكورة في أسباب أضغاث الأحلام . والثاني : أن يكون ذلك لأجل أن القوة المتخيلة ركبت لأجل ذلك المعنى صورة ثم ركبت لأجل تلك الصورة صورة ثانية ، وللثانية ثالثة وأمعنت في هذه الانتقالات، فانتهت بالآخرة إلى صورة لا تناسب المعنى الذي أدركته النفس أولاً البتة . وحينئذ يصير هذا القسم أيضاً من باب أضغاث الأحلام، ولهذا السبب قيل : إنه لا اعتماد على رؤية الكاذب والشاعر ، لأن القوة المتخيلة منهما قد تعودت الانتقالات الكاذبة الباطلة والله أعلم.
If you have understood this, then we say: When the soul obtains the slightest respite from the management of the body, it naturally connects to those principles, and some of those forms present with those principles become impressed in it, and these are the forms most suitable to that soul. It is known that the most suitable states for it are those pertaining to the states of that person, his companions, his countrymen, and his region. But if that person is inclined in aspiration towards acquiring the sciences of rational matters, things from them become manifest to him. And one whose aspiration is the interests of people sees them. Then when those forms are impressed in the substance of the rational soul, the imaginative faculty, which by nature imitates things, takes to depicting those forms impressed in the soul by means of particular forms that correspond to them. Then those forms become impressed in the common sense and become observable. This is the cause of dreams in sleep. Then those forms constructed by the imaginative faculty for the sake of those meanings may be strongly appropriate to those meanings, so this dream is independent of interpretation; sometimes they are not like that, yet they are also appropriate to those meanings in some respects, and here these dreams need interpretation. The benefit of interpretation is analysis in reverse, meaning that the interpreter goes from these forms present in the imagination to those meanings. As for the third division, that these forms are not at all appropriate to those meanings. This occurs for one of two reasons: First, that the occurrence of this strange imagination is due to one of the three mentioned causes of confused dreams. Second, that it is because the imaginative faculty constructed, for that meaning, a form, then for that form it constructed a second form, and for the second a third, and it went deep into these transitions, finally ending at a form that does not at all correspond to the meaning that the soul first apprehended. Then this division also becomes part of confused dreams. For this reason it is said: No reliance is placed on the dream of a liar or a poet, because the imaginative faculty of both has become accustomed to false and invalid transitions. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage classifies dreams (ar-ru'ya) and explains their causes. It begins by describing how, when the soul is free from bodily management, it connects to higher principles and receives forms suitable to its condition. The forms received relate to the person's own affairs or interests. Then the imaginative faculty (al-quwwa al-mutakhayyila) translates these abstract forms into particular sensory images that appear in the common sense (al-hiss al-mushtarak), making them visible as dreams. The passage then identifies three types of dreams based on the relationship between the constructed image and its original meaning: (1) strongly appropriate images that need no interpretation; (2) partially appropriate images requiring interpretation, which works by reversing the process from image to meaning; (3) images entirely inappropriate. The inappropriate type arises from two causes: either the image comes from one of the three causes of confused dreams (adghath al-ahlam), or it results from a chain of successive image constructions that lose connection to the original meaning. This latter case also counts as confused dreams, explaining why unreliable people's dreams are not trusted.

Key Terms
النفس الناطقة
القوة المتخيلة
الحس المشترك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dreams are direct messages from external sources without any mediation by the soul's faculties.
Correction: Razi explains that dreams involve the soul's connection to principles and subsequent construction by the imaginative faculty.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the first step in the process of dreaming?
A) The imaginative faculty constructs images
B) The soul connects to principles
C) The common sense impresses forms
D) The body manages itself

Key Takeaway
Dreams originate from forms impressed in the rational soul, which are then depicted by the imaginative faculty and made observable in the common sense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi says the soul receives forms most suitable to it based on a person's concerns and aspirations. When you look at the most vivid dreams you've had, what do they reveal about what your soul is truly attached to? Are you comfortable with that attachment, or does it expose a hidden inclination you have ignored?
?
The imaginative faculty depicts the received forms. If your dreams often contain fearful or impure images, what does that suggest about the state of your soul's impressions? What can you actively do during your waking hours to ensure your soul receives nobler forms?
?
You cannot control your dreams directly, but Razi implies that your aspirations and daily concerns shape the forms your soul receives. What is the single most important aspiration you want to imprint on your soul? What concrete step will you take today to make that aspiration more dominant than your worldly distractions?

Three Types of Dreams: From Clear to Confused
Razi classifies dreams into three categories and explains the causes of confused dreams.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's classification of dreams into three types based on the appropriateness of the dream image to its original meaning, and the role of interpretation.

Core Idea
Dreams are classified by how well the dream image matches its underlying meaning: some need no interpretation, some require it, and some are meaningless.

Having explained the basic process of dreams, Razi now classifies them based on the relationship between the constructed image and the original meaning. He identifies three types: those whose images are strongly appropriate and need no interpretation, those that are partially appropriate and require interpretation, and those that are completely inappropriate and are considered confused dreams. This lesson explores this classification and the causes of confused dreams.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Identify the three types. Notice how each is defined by the degree of appropriateness.
The passage uses 'قد تكون شديدة المناسبة' and 'قد لا تكون كذلك' and 'لا تكون مناسبة البتة'.
2
Understand the first two types
The first type needs no interpretation (غنية عن التعبير); the second needs interpretation (تحتاج إلى التعبير). Understand why interpretation is possible: analysis in reverse from image to meaning.
Interpretation works by reversing the process of construction.
3
Explore the third type and its causes
The third type is completely inappropriate. It arises from two causes: one of the three causes of confused dreams (adghath al-ahlam), or a chain of image constructions that lose connection. Notice the mention of poets and liars.
The imaginative faculty's habitual false transitions lead to unreliable dreams.
4
Reflect on the practical implications
Consider how this classification helps in evaluating dreams. Why are some dreams trustworthy and others not? How does interpretation work?
This classification has implications for dream interpretation practices.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : النفس إذا حصل لها أدنى فراغ من تدبير البدن ، اتصلت بطباعها بتلك المبادىء ، فتنطبع فيها بعض تلك الصور الحاضرة عند تلك المبادىء ، وهي الصورة التي هي أليق بتلك النفس . ومعلوم أن أليق الأحوال بها ، ما يتعلق بأحوال ذلك الإنسان وبأصحابه وأهل بلده وإقليمه . وأما إن كان ذلك الإنسان منجذب الهمة إلى تحصيل علوم المعقولات ، لاحت له منها أشياء . ومن كانت همته مصالح الناس رآها ، ثم إذا انطبعت تلك الصور في جوهر النفس الناطقة أخذت المتخيلة التي من طباعها محاكاة الأمور ، في حكاية تلك الصور المنطبعة في النفس ، بصور جزئية تناسبها ، ثم إن تلك الصور تنطبع في الحس المشترك فتصير مشاهدة. فهذا هو سبب الرؤيا في المنام . ثم إن تلك الصور التي ركبتها القوة المتخيلة ، لأجل تلك المعاني قد تكون شديدة المناسبة لتلك المعاني ، فتكون هذه الرؤيا غنية عن التعبير وقد لا تكون كذلك ، إلا أنها أيضاً مناسبة لتلك المعاني من بعض الوجوه ، وههنا تحتاج هذه المنامات إلى التعبير . وفائدة التعبير : التحليل بالعكس ، يعني : أن المعبر عن هذه الصور الحاضرة في الخيال إلى تلك المعاني وأما القسم الثالث أن لا تكون هذه الصور مناسبة لتلك المعاني البتة . وذلك يكون لأحد وجهين : أحدهما : أن يكون حدوث هذا الخيال الغريب ، إنما كان لوجه من الوجوه الثلاثة المذكورة في أسباب أضغاث الأحلام . والثاني : أن يكون ذلك لأجل أن القوة المتخيلة ركبت لأجل ذلك المعنى صورة ثم ركبت لأجل تلك الصورة صورة ثانية ، وللثانية ثالثة وأمعنت في هذه الانتقالات، فانتهت بالآخرة إلى صورة لا تناسب المعنى الذي أدركته النفس أولاً البتة . وحينئذ يصير هذا القسم أيضاً من باب أضغاث الأحلام، ولهذا السبب قيل : إنه لا اعتماد على رؤية الكاذب والشاعر ، لأن القوة المتخيلة منهما قد تعودت الانتقالات الكاذبة الباطلة والله أعلم.
If you have understood this, then we say: When the soul obtains the slightest respite from the management of the body, it naturally connects to those principles, and some of those forms present with those principles become impressed in it, and these are the forms most suitable to that soul. It is known that the most suitable states for it are those pertaining to the states of that person, his companions, his countrymen, and his region. But if that person is inclined in aspiration towards acquiring the sciences of rational matters, things from them become manifest to him. And one whose aspiration is the interests of people sees them. Then when those forms are impressed in the substance of the rational soul, the imaginative faculty, which by nature imitates things, takes to depicting those forms impressed in the soul by means of particular forms that correspond to them. Then those forms become impressed in the common sense and become observable. This is the cause of dreams in sleep. Then those forms constructed by the imaginative faculty for the sake of those meanings may be strongly appropriate to those meanings, so this dream is independent of interpretation; sometimes they are not like that, yet they are also appropriate to those meanings in some respects, and here these dreams need interpretation. The benefit of interpretation is analysis in reverse, meaning that the interpreter goes from these forms present in the imagination to those meanings. As for the third division, that these forms are not at all appropriate to those meanings. This occurs for one of two reasons: First, that the occurrence of this strange imagination is due to one of the three mentioned causes of confused dreams. Second, that it is because the imaginative faculty constructed, for that meaning, a form, then for that form it constructed a second form, and for the second a third, and it went deep into these transitions, finally ending at a form that does not at all correspond to the meaning that the soul first apprehended. Then this division also becomes part of confused dreams. For this reason it is said: No reliance is placed on the dream of a liar or a poet, because the imaginative faculty of both has become accustomed to false and invalid transitions. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage classifies dreams (ar-ru'ya) and explains their causes. It begins by describing how, when the soul is free from bodily management, it connects to higher principles and receives forms suitable to its condition. The forms received relate to the person's own affairs or interests. Then the imaginative faculty (al-quwwa al-mutakhayyila) translates these abstract forms into particular sensory images that appear in the common sense (al-hiss al-mushtarak), making them visible as dreams. The passage then identifies three types of dreams based on the relationship between the constructed image and its original meaning: (1) strongly appropriate images that need no interpretation; (2) partially appropriate images requiring interpretation, which works by reversing the process from image to meaning; (3) images entirely inappropriate. The inappropriate type arises from two causes: either the image comes from one of the three causes of confused dreams (adghath al-ahlam), or it results from a chain of successive image constructions that lose connection to the original meaning. This latter case also counts as confused dreams, explaining why unreliable people's dreams are not trusted.

Key Terms
القوة المتخيلة
الرؤيا
أضغاث الأحلام
التعبير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All dreams are either entirely true or entirely false; there is no middle ground.
Correction: Razi identifies three types: some are clear and need no interpretation, some need interpretation (partially true), and some are confused and unreliable.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the benefit (fa'ida) of dream interpretation (ta'bir)?
A) To confirm the dream's truth
B) To analyze the dream in reverse from image to meaning
C) To change the dream's meaning
D) To ignore confused dreams

Key Takeaway
Dreams are classified into three types based on the appropriateness of the dream image to its original meaning, with confused dreams arising from external causes or a chain of image constructions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you have a confusing dream, do you immediately dismiss it as meaningless or seek to understand its possible origin? How does Razi's classification challenge your reaction?
?
Razi says liars and poets have unreliable dreams because their imaginative faculty is accustomed to false transitions. Reflect: In what areas of your own thinking might you be accustomed to false transitions, leading you to conclusions that don't match reality?
?
Consider a dream you've had that you thought was 'just a dream.' Using Razi's three types, where would it fall? What does that say about your own state of mind or soul?

Magic Based on Imagination and Soul Purification
Definition and explanation of magic based on imagination and soul purification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how magic based on imagination and soul purification works according to the rational soul's connection to celestial spirits.

Core Idea
Focused imagination and purified souls draw power from celestial spirits to produce extraordinary effects.

This passage introduces the second type of magic, which relies not on physical substances but on the inner power of the soul. By understanding the soul's connection to celestial spirits and the effect of concentration, we gain insight into how such magic is believed to operate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the two key components: imagination (wahm) and soul purification (taṣfiyat al-nafs).
The passage is a continuous argument, not a list.
2
Understand the premise
The author states that rational souls are of the same kind as celestial spirits and generated from them. This is the foundation for the soul's inherent power.
3
Grasp the two causes
Identify the first cause: focused attention strengthens the soul's power. Then the second cause: through purification, the soul attracts power from its celestial origin.
These two causes can operate together or separately.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني من أنواع السحر : السحر المبني على قوة الوهم وتصفية النفس وتقريره : أنه ثبت في علم النفس : أن النفوس الناطقة من جنس الأرواح الفلكية ، ومولدة منها. وكأنها قطرات من تلك البحار ، وشموع من تلك الشموس ، فلا محالة يكون لها أثر ما ، وقوة ما ، فإذا أعرضت النفس الناطقة عن التوجه إلى سائر الجوانب، وبقيت متوجهة إلى غرض واحد ، ومطلوب معين ، قويت حينئذ قوتها ، واشتد تأثيرها ، فقدرت على إحداث أحوال غريبة في هذا العالم . وفيه سبب آخر : وهو أنها في حال الصفاء والرياضة تنجذب إلى الروح الفلكي، الذي هو طباعها التام ، وأبـوهـا الأصلي ، فسيرى من تلك الطباع التام ، قوة إلى جوهر النفس فيعظم لهذا السبب شأنها ، ويقوي تأثيرها
The second type of magic: magic based on the power of imagination (wahm) and purification of the soul. Its explanation: it is established in psychology that the rational souls are of the same kind as the celestial spirits (al-arwāḥ al-falakiyya) and are generated from them. They are like drops from those seas and candles from those suns, so inevitably they have some effect and some power. When the rational soul turns away from attending to all other directions and remains focused on a single purpose and a specific goal, its power then strengthens and its influence intensifies, so it becomes able to produce strange states in this world. And there is another cause: namely, that in the state of purity and spiritual exercise it is attracted to the celestial spirit, which is its complete nature and its original father; then from that complete nature a power flows to the substance of the soul, and for this reason its status becomes great and its influence strengthens.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage defines the second type of magic, which is based on the power of imagination (wahm) and purification of the soul (taṣfiyat al-nafs). The author first establishes a premise from psychology: rational souls belong to the same genus as celestial spirits and are generated from them, like drops from a sea or candles from the sun. Consequently, they inherently possess some effect and power. Then the explanation proceeds: when the soul withdraws from all other concerns and concentrates on a single goal, its power intensifies, enabling it to produce extraordinary phenomena. A second cause is added: through purity and spiritual exercises, the soul becomes attracted to its celestial origin, which is its complete nature and original father; from that source, power flows into the soul's substance, further magnifying its influence. Thus, the magic described here is an effect of focused imagination and spiritual purification, deriving its efficacy from the soul's connection to celestial spirits.

Key Terms
السحر
الوهم
تصفية النفس
النفس الناطقة
الأرواح الفلكية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what happens when the rational soul focuses on a single purpose?
A) It becomes disconnected from celestial spirits.
B) Its power weakens.
C) Its power strengthens and its influence intensifies.
D) It loses its ability to produce effects.

Key Takeaway
Magic based on imagination and soul purification works by focusing the soul's attention or purifying it to draw power from celestial spirits.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your soul is a drop from a celestial sea—do you live as if you draw from that infinite source, or have you let your focus scatter into worldly distractions that drain your spiritual power?
?
The text says that when the soul turns away from all other directions and fixes on a single purpose, its power strengthens. What one goal are you currently pursuing with such total concentration, and if none, what is keeping you from that level of sincerity?
?
Purification and spiritual exercise attract power from the celestial spirit—when was the last time you intentionally cleansed your soul of attachments to draw near to that original father? Are you willing to commit to a daily practice that refines your soul's substance?

Magic Based on Properties of Substances
Definition of magic based on properties of mineral, plant, and animal substances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third type of magic as defined by its reliance on the inherent properties of minerals, plants, animals, and even numbers.

Core Idea
Magic can work through the hidden properties of objects, not just spirits or imagination.

We now turn to the third type of magic, which relies not on imagination or spirit invocations but on the innate properties of physical substances. This passage from al-Matalib al-aliyah explains how minerals, plants, animals, and even numbers can be used for magical effects. It also points to a known work by Abū Bakr ibn Waḥshiyya on this subject.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation. Notice the concise definition of the third type.
Pay attention to the categories listed.
2
Identify the subcategories
The passage mentions several subcategories: properties of various stones, properties of animals generated through putrefaction, and also magic square numbers. List each one.
These all fall under the broad category of 'based on properties of medicinals'.
3
Reflect on putrefaction
Consider why the author includes animals produced by putrefaction. This indicates a belief that special properties can be harnessed from life forms generated through decay.
This shows that the category is broad and includes unusual sources.
4
Note the reference to Ibn Waḥshiyya
The author mentions having seen a book by Abū Bakr ibn Waḥshiyya on this topic. Consider how this reference adds authority and shows the tradition behind this classification.
Ibn Waḥshiyya was a known writer on occult sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثالث : السحر المبني على خواص الأدوية المعدنية والنباتية ، والحيوانية : ويدخل في هذا الباب خواص الأحجار المختلفة ، ويدخل فيه خواص الحيوانات التي نسعى في توليدها بطريق التعفنيات ، وقد يكون لكل جزء من أجزاء تلك الحيوانات آثار خاصة . ورأيت كتاباً في هذا الباب من مصنفات أبي بكر بن وحشية [ ويدخل في هذا الباب : السحر المبني على خواص اعداد الوفق ]
The third type: magic based on the properties (khavāṣṣ) of mineral, plant, and animal medicinals. Included in this category are the properties of various stones, and also the properties of animals that we seek to generate through putrefactions, and each part of those animals may have special effects. I have seen a book on this subject among the works of Abū Bakr ibn Waḥshiyya [and included in this category is magic based on the properties of magic square numbers].
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage defines the third type of magic, which relies on the specific properties (khavāṣṣ) of substances: minerals, plants, and animals. The author explains that this category includes the properties of precious stones, as well as the properties of animals produced through putrefaction (a process of generating life from decaying matter). Notably, each part of such animals may have its own special effects. The author also mentions having seen a book on this topic by Abū Bakr ibn Waḥshiyya, a known author on occult sciences. An additional note indicates that this category also includes magic based on the properties of magic square numbers (a‘dād al-wafq). Thus, this type of magic exploits the natural or occult properties inherent in physical objects.

Key Terms

الخواص
الأدوية المعدنية
النباتية
الحيوانية
الحجار
التعفنيات
أبي بكر بن وحشية
أعداد الوفق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All magic is based on imagination or spirit invocation.
Correction: This passage shows that a whole category of magic relies on the inherent properties of physical substances, not on spirits or imagination.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the third type of magic according to this passage?
A) Imagination and soul purification
B) Properties of mineral, plant, and animal substances
C) Invocation of spirits
D) Sleight of hand

Key Takeaway
Magic, in its third type, derives its power from the inherent properties of minerals, plants, animals, and even numbers, not from external spirits or human imagination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever attribute power or good fortune to an object—like a lucky charm, a stone, or a number—rather than to Allah alone? How does this lesson's description of magic based on properties challenge you to examine hidden shirk in your heart? What will you do to purify your trust in Allah only?
?
The author mentions animals generated through putrefaction and magic squares as sources of magical effects. What does this reveal about the human tendency to seek power from forbidden or corrupt sources? Where in your own life do you look for power or control apart from Allah, and what step can you take today to repent and rely solely on Him?
?
This lesson defines a type of magic that uses the inherent properties of creation. How comfortable are you with the idea that created things can have effects beyond the natural? Does this knowledge make you fear or respect these things, or does it strengthen your reliance on the Creator who gave them their properties? How will you guard your heart from being fascinated by such occult knowledge?

The Fourth Type of Magic: Incantations and Lower Spirits
Definition of magic based on incantations and lower spirits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the fourth type of magic is defined and its reliance on incantations and seeking help from jinn and devils.

Core Idea
Incantation magic relies on summoning lower spirits, not on physical tricks.

In this passage, the author identifies the fourth category of magic, which is fundamentally different from illusion or manipulation of natural properties. This type is built on specific incantations and the invocation of jinn and devils. Before reading, recall that the existence of such spirits has already been discussed in previous lessons.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic text and translation
Read the original and translation carefully, noting the key terms for incantations and spirits.
2
Identify the defining characteristics
Notice that this magic type is based on two elements: ritual formula (عزائم/رقي) and seeking aid from lower spirits (الأرواح السفلية).
The term 'علم التعزيمات' refers to the science of incantations.
3
Connect to prior discussion
The passage references a previous exhaustive discussion on affirming or denying such spirits. Consider why this reference matters for the present argument.
This implies the author has already established the existence of jinn and devils.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الرابع : السحر [ المبني على العزائم والرقي ] ويدخل في هذا الباب السحر [ المرتب على الاستعانة بالأرواح السفلية ، المسماة بالجن والشياطين؛ والكلام في إثبات هذا الجنس من الأرواح ونفيه قد سبق على الاستقصاء - وجمهور من يخوض في علم التعزيمات إنما يتمسكون بما في هذا الباب
The fourth type: magic [based on incantations and spells]. Included in this category is magic [based on seeking aid from lower spirits, called jinn and devils. The discussion of affirming or denying this kind of spirits has already been made exhaustively – and most of those who delve into the science of incantations only adhere to what is in this category.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth type of magic, which is founded on incantations (ʿazā’im) and spells (ruqā). The author then expands the definition by noting that this category includes magic that involves seeking help from lower spirits, specifically the jinn and devils. The author references a previous exhaustive discussion about the existence or non-existence of such spirits, implying that the present work already settled that issue. Furthermore, it is observed that most people engaged in the science of incantations (ʿilm al-taʿzīmāt) rely solely on this type of magic. Thus, this type is characterized by its reliance on supernatural beings through ritual formulas.

Magic Based on Seeking Aid from Celestial Spirits
Definition of magic based on celestial spirit aid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nature of the fifth type of magic that involves seeking help from celestial spirits and how it is believed to enable extraordinary feats.

Core Idea
Celestial spirits are widely accepted, and connecting with them is thought to grant miraculous powers.

This lesson explores a fascinating claim: that many cultures agree on the existence of celestial spirits and their powerful influence. Some even assert that through specific rituals, humans can ally with these spirits to perform wonders. We will examine the basis of this fifth type of magic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to understand the basic meaning.
Pay attention to the phrase 'khawāriq al-ʿādāt' (extraordinary acts).
2
Identify the key claim
The author states that most sects affirm celestial spirits and their great effects. Notice this consensus as the foundation for the magic type.
3
Understand the mechanism
The passage says that seeking aid through specific methods leads to connection with these spirits, granting ability to perform miracles.
This 'connection' is the crucial step.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الخامس من السحر : السحر المبني على الاستعانة بالأرواح الفلكية. فإنا قد بينا أن أكثر فرق أهل العالم مطبقون على إثبات هذه الأرواح ، وعلى أن لها آثاراً عظيمة في هذا العالم. وعند هذا قال بعضهم : إنه يمكن الاستعانة بها بطرق مخصوصة ، وإذا حصل ذلك الاتصال ، فقد حصلت القدرة على خوارق العادات
The fifth type of magic: magic based on seeking aid from celestial spirits. For we have shown that most sects of the people of the world are in agreement on affirming these spirits, and that they have great effects in this world. Upon this, some of them said: it is possible to seek aid from them through specific methods, and when that connection occurs, one obtains the ability to perform miracles (khawāriq al-ʿādāt).
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage defines the fifth type of magic, which relies on seeking assistance from celestial spirits (al-arwāḥ al-falakiyya). The author asserts that the majority of religious groups worldwide agree on the reality of these spirits and their significant influence in the world. Based on this consensus, some scholars claim that it is possible to connect with these spirits through special methods, and once this connection is achieved, the practitioner gains the power to perform extraordinary feats that break natural norms (khawāriq al-ʿādāt). Thus, this type of magic is essentially a form of spiritual cooperation with higher beings.

Key Terms
السحر
الاستعانة
الأرواح الفلكية
خوارق العادات

The Sixth Type of Magic: Sleight of Hand and Illusion
Definition of magic based on manual dexterity and illusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth type of magic as defined by the author: magic based on manual dexterity and illusion, distinct from supernatural forms.

Core Idea
Sleight of hand magic uses skill, not spirits, to deceive the eye.

The author now turns to the sixth type of magic, which relies not on supernatural forces but on human physical skill and visual trickery. This passage defines it clearly and introduces a specific variety called 'taking by the eyes.' The commentary will explain the significance of this type in distinguishing between different kinds of magic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then its literal translation. Let the author's own words introduce the concept.
2
Identify key elements
Notice the three components: manual dexterity (khiffat al-yad), sleight of hand (shuʿbadha), and showing what has no external existence.
These are all based on human skill.
3
Examine the sub-type
The passage mentions a variety called 'al-akhdh bi al-ʿuyūn' (taking by the eyes). Note that its explanation is promised later.
4
Contrast with previous types
Reflect on how this type differs from earlier magic types that involve spirits or natural properties. This one is purely physical trickery.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع السادس من السحر : ما يكون مرتباً على أعمال خفة اليد وطرق الشعبذة والسعي في إراءة مالا وجود له في الخارج ، ومنه نوع يقال له : الأخذ بالعيون. وسيأتي إن شاء الله تعالى تفسيره
The sixth type of magic: that which is based on acts of manual dexterity and methods of sleight of hand (shuʿbadha) and striving to show what has no external existence. Among them is a type called 'taking by the eyes' (al-akhdh bi al-ʿuyūn). Its explanation will come, God willing.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage defines the sixth type of magic, which is founded on physical skill and illusion. It involves manual dexterity (khiffat al-yad) and sleight of hand (shuʿbadha), with the aim of making the observer see things that do not actually exist. A specific variety within this type is called 'taking by the eyes' (al-akhdh bi al-ʿuyūn), likely a form of optical illusion or misdirection. The author promises to explain this further later. This type is essentially stage magic or illusionism, distinguished from other types by its reliance on human skill rather than supernatural forces.

Key Terms
السحر
خفة اليد
الشعبذة
الأخذ بالعيون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main characteristic of the sixth type of magic?
A) It uses celestial spirits.
B) It relies on the properties of minerals.
C) It shows things that have no external existence through dexterity.
D) It involves incantations.

Key Takeaway
The sixth type of magic is based on manual dexterity and illusion, aiming to show things that do not exist externally, and includes a variety called 'taking by the eyes'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You likely condemn magic, but when was the last time you used your own skills or words to create a false impression—to make yourself look better, to hide a mistake, or to manipulate someone's perception? Isn't that a form of the same deception this lesson warns about? Will you commit to truthfulness, even when it costs you?
?
The passage describes magic that shows 'what has no external existence'. What illusions in your life—status, wealth, relationships—do you pursue as if they are real and lasting, when in truth they are mere appearances that will vanish? How will you redirect your heart to what is truly eternal?
?
This sixth type of magic tricks the eyes but has no real power. Are you, like the audience of a magician, being fooled by the 'sleight of hand' of the world—distracted by glittering shows that hide emptiness? What specific deception in your daily life do you need to see through, starting today?

Magic Based on Geometry and Mechanics
Definition of magic based on geometry and engineering

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seventh category of magic as described by al-Isfahani, which is based on practical geometry and mechanics, involving moving heavy loads and vacuum-based devices.

Core Idea
Magic, in this tradition, includes sophisticated engineering that exploits natural principles like leverage and vacuum.

Al-Isfahani continues his classification of magic types. The seventh type is grounded not in illusion or demonic forces but in the mechanical sciences of geometry and physics. This passage introduces a category that blurs the line between magic and technology, showing how wondrous effects can be achieved through applied knowledge of natural laws.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Pay attention to the key terms: علم الهندسة (handasa), جر الأثقال (moving heavy loads), الخلاء (vacuum).
Notice that the author uses the word 'magic' for what we might call engineering.
2
Identify the two components
The passage mentions two sub-categories: (1) moving great weights with few instruments, and (2) wondrous works based on the necessity of a vacuum. Underline these in your mind.
The first refers to simple machines like levers and pulleys; the second to devices using air pressure like suction pumps.
3
Reflect on why this is considered magic
Ask yourself why al-Isfahani classifies these mechanical arts as a type of magic. The likely answer is that the underlying principles were not widely understood, so their effects appeared magical.
This highlights a broader theme: magic often refers to knowledge that is hidden or esoteric.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع السابع : السحر المرتب على علم الهندسة : ويدخل فيه علم جر الأثقال العظيمة بالآلات القليلة ، ومن جملتها : الأعمال العجيبة الغريبة ، المبنية على ضرورة الخلاء
The seventh type: magic based on the science of geometry (handasa). It includes the science of moving great weights with few instruments, and among them are the wondrous, strange works based on the necessity of a vacuum (khalā’).
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage defines the seventh type of magic, which is connected to the science of geometry (handasa) – essentially mechanics and engineering. The author explains that this category encompasses the technique of moving heavy loads using simple machines (like levers and pulleys). Furthermore, it includes amazing and bizarre contraptions that rely on the principle of the vacuum (khalā’), referring to devices that operate by creating a void, such as suction pumps. Thus, this type of magic is actually applied physics and engineering, producing effects that appear magical to the uninitiated.

Key Terms
السحر
علم الهندسة
جر الأثقال
الآلات
الخلاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What principle is mentioned as the basis for some wondrous works in the seventh type of magic?
A) Imagination
B) Properties of stones
C) Necessity of a vacuum
D) Sleight of hand

Key Takeaway
The seventh type of magic is essentially applied physics and engineering, demonstrating that marvels can be produced through understanding natural laws.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness an extraordinary event or a marvel of engineering, do you tend to see it as mere 'magic' or do you recognize the ordered natural laws Allah has placed in creation? How might this affect your appreciation of His wisdom?
?
This lesson shows that some forms of 'magic' are actually based on geometry and mechanics. Are there areas in your life where you prefer mystery over understanding, perhaps avoiding the effort to learn about the natural world? What is one step you can take this week to better understand a natural phenomenon as a sign of Allah's design?

The Eighth Type of Magic: Augury and Its Seven Subdivisions
Enumeration of seven subdivisions of augury-based magic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the eighth type of magic based on augury and its seven subdivisions, including their names and classifications.

Core Idea
Augury-based magic includes seven divinatory sciences.

The author now introduces the eighth type of magic, which is based on augury (fa’l) and omens (zajr). This type is further divided into seven specific sciences, ranging from physiognomy to bird flight interpretation. This lesson enumerates these subdivisions and explains their nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the eighth type of magic and its subdivisions.
Note that the passage is incomplete at the end.
2
Identify the seven subdivisions
List the seven sciences mentioned, noting that the first one (physiognomy) has two branches.
3
Observe the variety of methods
Notice that some subdivisions are based on interpreting physical features (lines, moles), while others involve actions (striking stones, bird flight).
4
Reflect on the classification
Ask yourself why the author considers these as subtypes of magic based on augury and omens.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثامن : السحر المبني على الفأل والزجر . وهذا النوع أيضاً أقسام : فأحدها وهو أجودها . علم الفراسة . وهو قسمان : فراسة روحانية ، وفراسة جسمانية . وثانيها : علم الرمل وثالثها : علم اختلاج الأعضاء ورابعها : علم النظر في الأكتاف وخامسها : علم ضرب الأحجار ، على ما هو عادة بعض النسوان سادسها : علم النظر في الغضون ، والأسرة الموجودة في كف اليدين واخمص القدمين ، وكذا علم النظر في الخيلان الموجودة على ظاهر البدن . وسابعها : العلم المأخوذ من كيفية طيران الطيور عن أوكارها، واعتبار
The eighth type: magic based on augury (fa’l) and omens (zajr). This type also has subdivisions: the first, and it is the most excellent, is the science of physiognomy (firāsa). It is of two kinds: spiritual physiognomy and physical physiognomy. The second is the science of sand divination (raml). The third is the science of tic (ikhtilāj al-a‘ḍā’). The fourth is the science of looking at shoulder blades (aktāf). The fifth is the science of striking stones (ḍarb al-āḥjār), as is the custom of some women. The sixth is the science of looking at the wrinkles and lines present in the palm of the hands and the sole of the feet, and likewise the science of looking at beauty marks (khīlān) present on the surface of the body. And the seventh is the knowledge derived from the manner of birds flying from their nests, and considering...
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage defines the eighth type of magic, which is based on augury (fa’l) and omens (zajr). The author then enumerates seven subdivisions within this type. The first and best is the science of physiognomy (firāsa), which itself has two branches: spiritual and physical. The second is geomancy (raml), divination using sand. The third is the science of interpreting muscle twitches (ikhtilāj al-a‘ḍā’). The fourth is scapulimancy, reading omens from shoulder blades. The fifth is lithomancy, or stone divination, particularly practiced by women. The sixth is chiromancy (palmistry) and podomancy, reading lines on hands and feet, as well as moles (khīlān) on the body. The seventh, which is incomplete in this passage, is divination based on the flight patterns of birds. This eighth type thus encompasses various forms of divination and folk prognostication.

علم النظر في الأكتاف
علم ضرب الأحجار
علم النظر في الغضون
الخيلان

Key Takeaway
The eighth type of magic comprises seven divinatory sciences, with physiognomy being the most excellent.

The Fifth Type of Magic: Seeking Help from Spirits
Defines the fifth type as spirit-based magic and states its premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the defining characteristic of the fifth type of magic as seeking help from spirits and the premise on which it is based.

Core Idea
Magic can involve seeking help from spirits to perform extraordinary feats.

The author has introduced several types of magic, and now we reach the fifth. This type moves beyond physical manipulation or deception and into the realm of spirit interaction. The passage presents the premise that most people affirm the existence of spirits and their power, and then reports the claim that specific methods can harness that power. Let's examine the text closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: a definition, a premise, and a claim. The author uses 'we have shown' to refer to earlier discussion.
This is a reporting passage; the author is not necessarily endorsing the view.
2
Identify the key components
Identify the definition: 'magic based on seeking help from spirits.' Then find the premise: 'most sects...affirm these spirits and their great effects.' Finally, note the claim: 'through specific methods, connection is possible, leading to extraordinary feats.'
3
Reflect on the author's stance
Notice that the author does not say this type is true or false. He simply reports it as a view held by some. Ask yourself: why might the author include it if not endorsing it?
This connects to the common misunderstanding field below.

Source Text

النوع الخامس من السحر : السحر المبني على الاستعانة بالأرواح فإنا قد بينا أن أكثر فرق أهل العالم مطبقون على إثبات هذه الأرواح ، وعلى أن لها آثاراً عظيمة في هذا العالم. وعند هذا قال بعضهم : إنه يمكن الاستعانة بها بطرق مخصوصة ، وإذا حصل ذلك الاتصال ، فقد حصلت القدرة على خوارق العادات

Commentary
This passage defines the fifth type of magic. It begins by naming the type: magic based on seeking help from spirits. It then provides a premise: most people affirm the existence of spirits and believe they have great effects in the world. Based on that consensus, some claim that through specific methods, one can connect with these spirits and thereby gain the ability to perform extraordinary feats (khawariq al-'adat). The author presents this as a reported view without explicitly endorsing or refuting it.

Defines the sixth type of magic as illusion based on manual dexterity.

Core Idea
Sleight of hand magic deceives the eyes with skill, not supernatural power.

The author now turns to the sixth category of magic, which is based not on hidden forces but on physical dexterity and optical illusion. This type includes sleight of hand, juggling, and a specific form called 'al-akhdh bil-'uyun' (taking with the eyes). Let's read the passage to see how it is defined.

3
Reflect on the nature of this magic
Consider how this type differs from earlier types of magic. It does not rely on spirits or hidden forces but purely on physical skill and visual deception.
The author mentions a subtype 'al-akhdh bil-'uyun' which may involve hypnosis or eye-catching tricks.

Mechanical marvels are natural, not supernatural.

The Eighth Type of Magic: Omens and Augury
Defines the eighth type as based on omens and lists eight divinatory practices.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the eighth type of magic as defined by the author, which is based on omens and augury, and its eight subdivisions including physiognomy, geomancy, and others.

Core Idea
Omens and augury form the basis of eight magical arts, each predicting fortune.

This passage categorizes the eighth type of magic, which focuses on interpreting signs and omens. The author lists eight distinct practices, from physiognomy to general augury, showing how diverse divinatory methods were considered part of magic. Understanding this classification helps us see the breadth of magical arts in the tradition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Note the structure: a definition followed by eight numbered subdivisions.
2
Identify the main type and its subdivisions
The passage defines the eighth type as based on omens (الفأل) and augury (الزجر). Then it lists eight subdivisions. Find each one and see how the author describes it.
The first subdivision is said to be the best.
3
Understand each subdivision
Go through each of the eight subdivisions. Notice the variety: some involve the body (physiognomy, twitching, moles), others involve objects (sand, stones, shoulder blades), and others involve animals (birds).
The last one is general augury from all events.
4
Reflect on the classification
Ask yourself why these practices are classified as magic. How does the author's classification reflect the worldview of the time?
This connects to the common misunderstanding that these are legitimate sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثامن : السحر المبني على الفأل والزجر . وهذا النوع أيضاً أقسام : فأحدها وهو أجودها . علم الفراسة . وهو قسمان : فراسة روحانية ، وفراسة جسمانية . وثانيها : علم الرمل وثالثها : علم اختلاج الأعضاء ورابعها : علم النظر في الأكتاف وخامسها : علم ضرب الأحجار ، على ما هو عادة بعض النسوان سادسها : علم النظر في الغضون ، والأسرة الموجودة في كف اليدين واخمص القدمين ، وكذا علم النظر في الخيلان الموجودة على ظاهر البدن . وسابعها : العلم المأخوذ من كيفية طيران الطيور عن أوكارها، واعتبار السانج والبارح منها ، وكيفية أصواتها عند التوجه إلى الإنسان ، وعند الانصراف عنه . وثامنها : علم التفاؤل بجميع أنواع الحوادث في معرفة العسر واليسر
The eighth type: magic based on omens and augury. And this type also has divisions: the first of them, and it is the best, is physiognomy (al-firasa). And it is of two kinds: spiritual physiognomy and physical physiognomy. The second: the science of sand (al-raml). The third: the science of involuntary muscle twitching. The fourth: the science of looking at shoulder blades. The fifth: the science of striking stones, as is the custom of some women. The sixth: the science of looking at wrinkles and lines present in the palm of the hands and the soles of the feet, and likewise the science of looking at moles present on the surface of the body. The seventh: the knowledge taken from the manner of birds flying from their nests, and considering the 'sanij' and 'barih' among them, and the manner of their sounds when approaching a person and when departing from him. The eighth: the science of auguring by all types of events in knowing difficulty and ease.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage defines the eighth type of magic and enumerates its eight subdivisions. The main type is based on omens (الفأل) and augury (الزجر). The first and best subdivision is physiognomy (علم الفراسة), which itself has two kinds: spiritual and physical. The second is geomancy (علم الرمل). The third is the study of involuntary muscle twitches. The fourth is shoulder blade reading. The fifth is stone throwing, associated with women. The sixth is palmistry and foot reading, including moles. The seventh is bird flight interpretation, distinguishing between the 'sanij' and 'barih' (perhaps left- and right-side flying birds). The eighth is general augury from all events to predict fortune. This classification shows how various divinatory arts were considered magical.

Misconception: These practices are considered legitimate sciences or harmless superstitions.
Correction: In this classification, they are explicitly identified as types of magic, indicating they were viewed as forbidden or occult arts.

The Ninth Type: Drugging Food for Mind Control
Defines ninth type as food-based mind control.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ninth type of magic as a form of mental control through drugging food, and why it is considered a physical rather than supernatural effect.

Core Idea
Drugging food to control minds is physical manipulation, not supernatural magic.

The author now describes a more insidious form of magic: one that relies on physical substances to impair the victim's reason and will. This ninth type is not about illusions or spirits but about chemically altering a person's mental state. As you read, notice how the text presents this as a straightforward cause-and-effect mechanism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own description come through.
2
Identify the three effects
The passage lists three specific effects of the food on the victim: deficiency of intellect, weakness of opinion, and dullness of nature. Find each one in the Arabic.
These are progressive impairments affecting cognition and will.
3
Understand the purpose
After the effects, the passage states the goal: to make the person easy to manage and move as desired. This shows the manipulative aim of this magic type.
4
Reflect on its nature
Consider why this is considered magic despite being a physical substance effect. This highlights the blurred line between magic and science in historical contexts.
The common misunderstanding is that all magic is supernatural.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع التاسع من السحر : المبني على إطعام الطعام ، الذي يوجب قلة العقل ، وضعف الرأي وبلادة الطبع . ثم إذا صار الإنسان كذلك فحينئذ يسهل تصريفه وتحريكه كيف أريد
The ninth type of magic: based on feeding food that causes deficiency of intellect, weakness of opinion, and dullness of nature. Then when a person becomes like that, it becomes easy to manage and move him however one wishes.

Once the victim's mental faculties are compromised, the magician can easily control and direct him as desired. This is essentially a form of poisoning or drugging used for manipulation.

The Tenth Type of Magic: Lies, Deceit, and Fear
Defines the tenth type as based on lies, deceit, and instilling fear.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the tenth type of magic, which relies on psychological manipulation through lies, deceit, and fear.

Core Idea
Magic can work by exploiting fear and deception, not supernatural powers.

In this lesson, we explore the tenth type of magic as described in the text. This type is distinct because it operates through psychological means—spreading lies, employing deceit, and most importantly, instilling fear in the heart. The author explains how once fear dominates, the magician can easily promote any falsehood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the concise definition style.
The passage is short but packs three key elements.
2
Identify the core methods
List the three components mentioned: spreading lies, types of deceit, and ways that cause fear.
These are not supernatural but psychological.
3
Understand the role of fear
Note that fear is emphasized—it is the enabler that makes deception easy.
The phrase 'when fear takes over' shows the mechanism.
4
Reflect on psychological manipulation
Consider how this differs from other magic types that might use physical or spiritual means.
This type targets the heart/mind.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع العاشر : السحر المبني على ترويج الأكاذيب وأنواع المكر والخداع . ومنه : الطرق التي توجب تسليط الخوف على القلب . وعند استيلاء الخوف ، يسهل ترويج أي شيء أريد
The tenth type: magic based on spreading lies and types of deceit and deception. And among it: the ways that cause fear to overcome the heart. And when fear takes over, it becomes easy to promote anything one wishes.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage defines the tenth type of magic. It relies on spreading falsehoods, deception, and trickery. A key method is inducing fear in the heart. Once fear dominates, the magician can easily convince the victim of anything. This type uses psychological manipulation rather than physical or spiritual means.

Summarizes the ten types, dismisses extreme claims, and highlights reliance on astrological magic.

Small Size Does Not Negate Influence
Refutes the objection that small celestial bodies have no power or influence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author disproves the claim that small celestial bodies cannot have influence.

Core Idea
Small size does not negate influence; even imaginary points can have effects.

The objection that small celestial bodies cannot exert influence is common among those who equate physical size with power. In this passage, the author refutes this by citing examples from astrology itself: tiny Mercury, mathematical points like lunar nodes, and even imaginary points are all considered effective. This shows that influence is not tied to size.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the initial statement of the objection: 'Because their smallness necessitates their weakness.' Then see the author's sharp refutation: 'This is false.'
The author does not simply argue; he provides concrete examples.
2
Examine the Mercury example
Mercury is very small yet comparable to larger planets. Reflect on why this undermines the objection.
Mercury's smallness does not prevent it from being compared to Jupiter or Saturn.
3
Consider the lunar nodes
The 'head' and 'tail' are mere points, but astrologers attribute great effects to them. This shows that even non-physical points can be influential.
4
Consider imaginary points
Fortune and the 'arrow of the unseen' are purely imaginary, yet astrologers ascribe strong effects to them. This is the strongest blow to the objection.
If imaginary points have influence, physical size is irrelevant.
5
Synthesize the argument
Understand that the objection fails because it assumes a link between size and influence that is not supported by astrological practice itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لها قوة وتأثير أصلاً . لأجل أن صغرها يوجب ضعفها . فنقول : هذا باطل لأن عطارد مع غاية صغره ، تقاربه سائر السيارات مع عظم أجرامها . بل نقول : الرأس والذنب نقطتان ، وأصحاب الأحكام أثبتوا لهما آثاراً عظيمة بل نقول : السعادة ، وسهم الغيب نقطتان وهميتان ، والأحكاميون أثبتوا لهما آثاراً قوية .
They have power and influence originally. Because their smallness necessitates their weakness. So we say: This is false, because Mercury, despite its extreme smallness, is comparable to the other planets despite the magnitude of their bodies. Rather we say: the head and the tail are two points, and the astrologers have affirmed for them great effects. Rather we say: fortune and the arrow of the unseen are two imaginary points, and the astrologers have affirmed for them strong effects.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage refutes an objection that small celestial bodies, due to their small size, cannot have influence. The author argues that this is false, citing Mercury as an example: though very small, it is comparable to larger planets. He then points out that astrologers themselves attribute significant effects to the 'head' and 'tail' of the moon (the nodes) which are merely points, and to imaginary points like 'fortune' and 'arrow of the unseen'. Thus, smallness does not negate influence.

Proof from the Milky Way: Stars Cannot Be Counted
Proves unknowable star count using Milky Way.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the appearance of the Milky Way demonstrates that the total number of stars is unknowable.

Core Idea
The Milky Way's cloudy appearance proves we cannot count all the stars.

The author presents a second argument about the impossibility of knowing the exact number of celestial lights. He uses a specific astronomical observation — the Milky Way — as evidence. This passage shows how careful reasoning from observable phenomena can lead to theological conclusions about the limits of human knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the phrase 'ثبت بالدليل' (established by proof), indicating this is a reasoned argument.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the premise and conclusion
The premise is that the Milky Way is composed of many small stars that appear as a cloudy patch. The conclusion is that complete knowledge of the number of stars is impossible. Find these two parts in the text.
3
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider why this argument matters in a theological context. It guards against claiming to have exhaustive knowledge of Allah's creation, promoting humility.
4
Address the common misunderstanding
Some think the Milky Way is a nebula or a single object. The author clarifies it is a dense cluster of stars. Understand this corrects a scientific misconception.
This is mentioned in the passage: 'ليست إلا أجراما كوكبية صغيرة الحجم'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني : في بيان أن عدد النيرات الفلكية غير معلومة ، وهو أنه ثبت بالدليل أن المجرة ، ليست إلا أجراما كوكبية صغيرة الحجم ، مرتكزة في فلك الثوابت ، إلا أنها لصغرها لا يتميز بعضها عن البعض ، فنشاهد جملتها على صورة لطخة سحابية ، وهذا يدل على أن الوقوف التام على معرفة أعداد الكواكب : ممتنع الحصول .
The second aspect: in explaining that the number of celestial lights is unknown, and it is that it has been established by proof that the Milky Way is nothing but small planetary bodies, fixed in the sphere of the fixed stars, except that due to their smallness they are not distinguished from each other, so we perceive them as a whole in the form of a cloudy patch. And this indicates that complete knowledge of the number of stars is impossible to obtain.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage introduces the second argument (al-wajh al-thani) demonstrating the difficulty of knowing the number of stars. The author states that the Milky Way is actually composed of many small stars, but because they are so small and close together, they appear as a cloudy patch. This observable fact proves that we cannot have complete knowledge of the count of all stars, as many are hidden from us.

Correction: The Milky Way is actually composed of countless small stars that are not individually distinguishable, forming a cloudy patch.

Spots on the Moon and Sun as Evidence of Unknown Stars
Author cites lunar and solar spots as evidence for countless stars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how celestial observations of spots on the moon and sun are used to argue that human intellect cannot comprehend the total number of stars.

Core Idea
Spots on the moon and sun reveal hidden stars, proving our intellect's limits.

This passage presents a fascinating argument from celestial observations. Scholars point to the faint patches on the moon and a black spot near the sun's center as evidence that many stars exist beyond our perception. The author uses these phenomena to demonstrate the finite capacity of human reason when faced with the vastness of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Focus on the two examples given.
2
Identify the two pieces of evidence
Notice the moon spots and the sun spot are presented separately. Ask: what do they have in common?
Both are normally invisible but indicate hidden celestial bodies.
3
Grasp the conclusion
The author concludes that these observations show human intellects cannot count all stars. Reflect on why this is a logical step.
The unseen stars are real but beyond our counting ability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إن المحققين ذهبوا إلى أن المحو ، المحسوس في وجه القمر ، إنما حصل بسبب أن كواكب صغيرة ارتكزت في وجه القمر . وقال آخرون : إنه حصل في وجه الشمس فوق النقطة التي هي كالمركز لقرص الشمس : نقطة سوداء ، كالخال في وجه الشمس . إلا أنها لا تظهر لأجل قوة نور الشمس . وقد تعرض عوارض مخصوصة. تصير تلك النقطة السوداء كالمشاهدة ، وكل هذه الاعتبارات تدل على أن العقول البشرية ، قاصرة عن الإحاطة بعدد نيرات الأفلاك
The third: The investigators have held that the faintness (mahw) perceived in the face of the moon occurred because small stars are fixed in the face of the moon. And others said: In the face of the sun, above the point that is like the center of the sun’s disk, there is a black spot like a mole on the face of the sun. However, it does not appear due to the strength of the sun’s light. And it may be subject to specific conditions that make that black spot observable. And all these considerations indicate that human intellects are incapable of encompassing the number of the stars of the spheres.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This third argument uses celestial observations to show human cognitive limits. The author notes that scholars attribute the faint patches on the moon to small stars embedded in it. Similarly, some claim there is a black spot near the center of the sun, like a mole, but it is usually hidden by the sun's brightness. Only under special conditions does it become visible. These phenomena demonstrate that many stars exist that we cannot see, so human reason cannot fully count the stars.

وجه القمر
نقطة سوداء
قرص الشمس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The spots themselves are stars.

Checkpoint

A) That the sun is imperfect
B) That human intellect cannot encompass the number of stars
C) That the sun has a solid surface
D) That stars can be seen during the day

Key Takeaway
Celestial spots reveal that many stars exist beyond our sight, proving human intellect cannot fully grasp the number of stars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
